<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer15505951489096335547">Historians and inquisitive laymen alike love to ponder the dramatic what-its of history. In these twenty never-before-published essays, some of the keenest minds of our time ask the big, tantalizing questions: Where might we be if history had not unfolded the way it did? Why, how, and when was our fortune made real? The answers are surprising, sometimes frightening, and al</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15505951489096335547">Historians and inquisitive laymen alike love to ponder the dramatic what-its of history. In these twenty never-before-published essays, some of the keenest minds of our time ask the big, tantalizing questions: Where might we be if history had not unfolded the way it did? Why, how, and when was our fortune made real? The answers are surprising, sometimes frightening, and always entertaining.. "In addition to the essays, fifteen sidebars by such authors as Caleb Carr, Tom Wicker, David Fromkin, and Ted Morgan illuminate in brief other world-changing episodes.<br /><br /><u>
  <em>
    <strong>Included Essays and Sidebars</strong>
  </em>
</u><br />  • <u>
  <em>The What Ifs of 1914</em>
</u> by Robert Cowley<br />          What if Great Britain had remained neutral in World War I?<br />  • <u>
  <em>Bismarck's Empire Stillborn</em>
</u> by James Chase  (sidebar)<br />          What if Bismarck hadn't won in 1879?<br />  • <u>
  <em>Thanks But No Cigar</em>
</u> by David Clay Large  (sidebar)<br />          What If Annie Oakley had shot Kaiser Wilhelm in a performance?<br />  • <u>
  <em>Armistice of Desperation</em>
</u> by Dennis E. Showalter (sidebar)<br />         What if combatants had been more aggressive? Fears promoted<br />          European unity, and revolutions were avoided?<br />  • <u>
  <em>How Hitler Could Have Won The War</em>
</u> by John Keegan<br />       What if the Wehrmacht had turned toward the Middle East<br />            instead of the Soviet Union?<br /><br /><br />  •  <u>
  <em>Infectious Alternatives</em>
</u> by William H. McNeill<br />       What if a plague had not forced the Assyrians to withdraw from their<br />           siege of Jerusalem in 701 BC?<br />  • <u>
  <em>No Glory That Was Greece</em>
</u> by Victor Davis Hanson<br />       What if the Persians had won the Battle of Salamis?<br />  • <u>
  <em>Conquest Denied</em>
</u> by Josiah Ober<br />       What if Alexander the Great had died at the<br />            Battle of the Granicus River?<br />   • <u>
  <em>Furor Teutonicus: The Teutoburg Forest, A.D. 9</em>
</u> by Lewis H. Lapham<br />       What if Varus had defeated Arminius at the<br />         Battle of the Teutoburg Forest?<br />  •  <u>
  <em>The Dark Ages Made Lighter</em>
</u> by Barry S. Strauss<br />       What if the battles of Adrianople in 378 and Poitiers in 732 had been<br />          won by the Romans and the Muslims, respectively?<br />  • <u>
  <em>The Death That Saved Europe</em>
</u> by Cecelia Holland<br />       What if Ogadai Khan had not died in 1241 on the eve of the<br />         Mongol siege of Vienna?<br />  • <u>
  <em>If Only It Had Not Been Such a Wet Summer</em>
</u> by Theodore K. Rabb<br />       What if Suleiman the Magnificent had begun his 1529 siege of<br />        Vienna earlier in the year?<br />  • <u>
  <em>The Immolation of Hernán Cortés</em>
</u> by Ross Hassig<br />       What if Cortés had been killed or his expedition into Aztec-dominated<br />        Mexico had failed? (The essay discusses La Noche Triste, the<br />        near-destruction of Cortés' force in 1520, as a key possibility<br />        of a point of divergence.)<br />  • <u>
  <em>The Repulse of the English Fireships</em>
</u> by Geoffrey Parker<br />       What if the Spanish Armada had successfully landed in England?<br />  •  <u>
  <em>Unlikely Victory</em>
</u> by Thomas Fleming<br />        What if the Americans lost the Revolutionary War?<br />            (Thirteen ways are presented here.)<br />  •  <u>
  <em>What the Fog Wrought</em>
</u> by David McCullough<br />          What if George Washington and his forces had not escaped after<br />                losing the Battle of Long Island?<br />  •  <u>
  <em>Ruler of the World</em>
</u> by Alistair Horne<br />         What if Napoleon Bonaparte had chosen to do<br />              several things differently?<br />  •  <u>
  <em>If the Lost Order Hadn't Been Lost</em>
</u> by James M. McPherson<br />         What if Robert E. Lee had been able to march through Pennsylvania<br />            and Maryland without fighting the Battle of Antietam?<br />  •  <u>
  <em>A Confederate Cannae and Other Scenarios</em>
</u> by Stephen W. Sears<br />         What if the Civil War had not lasted as long as it did?<br />            (Five ways are presented here.)<br />  • <u>
  <em>Our Midway Disaster</em>
</u> by Theodore F. Cook, Jr.<br />       What if the Japanese had won the Battle of Midway?<br />  • <u>
  <em>D Day Fails</em>
</u> by Stephen E. Ambrose<br />       What if the Allied invasion of Europe had failed in June 1944?<br />  • <u>
  <em>Funeral in Berlin</em>
</u> by David Clay Large<br />       What if American and not Soviet forces had taken Berlin in 1945?<br />  • <u>
  <em>China Without Tears</em>
</u> by Arthur Waldron<br />       What if the Chinese Civil War had ended with Chiang Kai-shek not<br />        marching to retake Manchuria from Mao Zedong &amp; the Communists?</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15505951489096335547">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1490307015?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 04, 2016</a>

      <a href="/user/show/502680-david" class="user" title="David ">David </a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/502680-david?shelf=history" class="actionLinkLite">history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1490307015">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12195217238830643353">The way the world is today came to be through a series of razor's edges (or hinges, or whatever cliche you want to use) throughout history. This is a fun book that deals with many of the most important turning points, and speculating on what might have happened if things turned out differently. Mostly, the world may have ended up unrecognizable to those of us living in this one - had the Muslims won at Poitiers we would all be Muslim, unless of course the Persians had won at Salamis which would</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12195217238830643353">The way the world is today came to be through a series of razor's edges (or hinges, or whatever cliche you want to use) throughout history. This is a fun book that deals with many of the most important turning points, and speculating on what might have happened if things turned out differently. Mostly, the world may have ended up unrecognizable to those of us living in this one - had the Muslims won at Poitiers we would all be Muslim, unless of course the Persians had won at Salamis which would have maybe meant Christianity and Islam did not rise at all. Likewise, had the Romans not lost in the Teuteborg forest or later at Adrianople, things may have turned out quite differently for Muslims and Christians. Lest we be dismayed, if the Mongol leader had not suddenly died, causing his generals to need to return home, the Mongols may have conquered all of Europe in 1242 putting an end to Islam, Christianity or whatever remnants of Persian or Roman religion existed. <br /><br />Yeah, that Mongol one was the most terrifying.<br /><br />What if Napolean had not invaded Russia, or even better not tried to take over the Iberian peninsula? Likewise, imagine Hitler choosing to strike east, following Alexander's route, rather than attacking Russia (why does anyone ever attack Russia?). He could have taken the oil fields he needed, linked up with Japan, and put himself in a position to strike at Russia from an easier route.<br /><br />After a while I did find myself skimming some of these essays, but if you like history this is a fun read.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12195217238830643353">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2199353322529439921">Enh. A bit of a disappointment, for all that it took me more than four years to get to it. I skimmed a lot of the essays dealing with earlier history, as there was much moaning about how if such-and-such battle had been lost or so-and-so had died earlier or later then GREEK CIVILIZATION NEVER WOULD HAVE DEVELOPED AND WE'D ALL BE RIDING HORSES OR WEARING VEILS AND LIVING IN POLICE STATES AND SPEAKING ASSYRIAN/PERSIAN/SOME GOD-AWFUL ORIENTAL LANGUAGE OH NOES!!!!11!!<br /><br />Right, *cough* color me unimpres</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2199353322529439921">Enh. A bit of a disappointment, for all that it took me more than four years to get to it. I skimmed a lot of the essays dealing with earlier history, as there was much moaning about how if such-and-such battle had been lost or so-and-so had died earlier or later then GREEK CIVILIZATION NEVER WOULD HAVE DEVELOPED AND WE'D ALL BE RIDING HORSES OR WEARING VEILS AND LIVING IN POLICE STATES AND SPEAKING ASSYRIAN/PERSIAN/SOME GOD-AWFUL ORIENTAL LANGUAGE OH NOES!!!!11!!<br /><br />Right, *cough* color me unimpressed. When there was some "what if" speculation, it was usually pretty grim and/or racist. Many of the historians seemed to think that things have turned pretty well, and that things going otherwise would have meant doom and despair for all civilization. As I'm fairly sure not everybody is happy with how history turned out, how about a little speculation on how we could have done better? For example, China might want a do-over for the past 200 years...or say, the Middle East for the past 3000.<br /><br />The later essays were better, though a better grasp of history than mine is recommended. I'll be checking out the sequel, if only because it's sitting right here. I do, however, recommend that if you're going to only read one essay out of this book, that it be the one about the Mongol horde.</span>

      <a href="/user/show/608830-pspealman" class="user" title="Pspealman">Pspealman</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer8856618">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18275781395678280345">I'm not one much for military history and yet this is easily one of the best history books I've read.<br /><br />It isn't that the writing is particularly great - there are no lines that beat you in the face with the sublime till you spout ramblings about Grecian urns. But it is consistently clear with an excitement over the subject matter that is infectious to the general reader. The selection of writers is excellent and the tone, mostly, consistent hovering somewhere between a textbook and a deranged time</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18275781395678280345">I'm not one much for military history and yet this is easily one of the best history books I've read.<br /><br />It isn't that the writing is particularly great - there are no lines that beat you in the face with the sublime till you spout ramblings about Grecian urns. But it is consistently clear with an excitement over the subject matter that is infectious to the general reader. The selection of writers is excellent and the tone, mostly, consistent hovering somewhere between a textbook and a deranged time traveling beltway think tanker. Maybe my appreciation of the book is because it isn't about history but the near-history. <br /><br />Like a Lipstick Traces of close calls, near misses, and improbable long shots that still panned out, the book careens through the turbulent times of our bloody world to land, safely at last, on the deeply disturbing idea that the world is happenstance, coincidence, error, and seriously dim bulbs with armament.<br /><br /></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_248923229">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/248923229" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3843117-ensiform" class="left imgcol" title="Ensiform"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1275973241p2/3843117.jpg" alt="Ensiform" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/248923229?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 19, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3843117-ensiform" class="user" title="Ensiform">Ensiform</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3843117-ensiform?shelf=non-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3843117-ensiform?shelf=war" class="actionLinkLite">war</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer248923229">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18264014682544222641">Replace “The World’s” in the subtitle with “American and British,” and you have an accurate description of the book. A series of essays, padded with a dozen or so one-page counterfactual presentations. An endlessly fascinating idea with great potential, unfortunately not fully realized. Through 2700 years of history the authors, with varying amounts of detail, and with varying degrees of success, review some great military turning points in history, and their alternate outcomes.<br /><br />One great flaw is</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18264014682544222641">Replace “The World’s” in the subtitle with “American and British,” and you have an accurate description of the book. A series of essays, padded with a dozen or so one-page counterfactual presentations. An endlessly fascinating idea with great potential, unfortunately not fully realized. Through 2700 years of history the authors, with varying amounts of detail, and with varying degrees of success, review some great military turning points in history, and their alternate outcomes.<br /><br />One great flaw is the lack of consistency in approach, layout, and thoroughness; Cowley is too lax an editor. Of particular note, some of the more well-known historians, like Keegan and Ambrose, present pieces that are so brief, and so lacking in academic rigor, one wonders if they weren't written on the back of a cocktail napkin. Others, like Josiah Ober’s essay which suggests a lasting, unified Roman-Persian empire (!!) simply if Alexander the Great had died early (!), are ridiculous. That said, there are also some truly outstanding pieces, such as Theodore Cook’s detailed, thoughtful consideration of American Pacific long-term strategy following a defeat at Midway, or Arthur Waldron’s speculation on a Nationalist China. About half of the one-pagers are good too, such as the one on the New York taxi that might have killed Winston Churchill. Overall, the essays provide good overviews of particular events, and at the very least, make you think about the whims of weather and men. Ultimately, this book is an enjoyable read, and a nice overview of military history. It cannot, however, be considered a work of serious scholarship. A few non-Western contributions, or some rebuttals of the sillier claims, might have given this book some weight. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="18264014682544222641">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/6647424?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 17, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/334806-liirogue" class="user" title="liirogue">liirogue</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer6647424">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10772570582632336379">A compilation of essays that look at how things might have gone if battles had turned out differently. I found some of the scenarios very interesting. Unfortunately, either my attention span or the quality of the book began to suffer as it continued. Since the later essays were close in time, they seemed to become rather repetitive. There were even occasions when the same battle was written about twice, which really began to push my boredom level.<br /><br />Overall, an interesting book, but it definitely c</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10772570582632336379">A compilation of essays that look at how things might have gone if battles had turned out differently. I found some of the scenarios very interesting. Unfortunately, either my attention span or the quality of the book began to suffer as it continued. Since the later essays were close in time, they seemed to become rather repetitive. There were even occasions when the same battle was written about twice, which really began to push my boredom level.<br /><br />Overall, an interesting book, but it definitely could have used some diversification. It got to where I could predict some of the theories of where history may have gone if X had been changed - they needed to mix the formula up a bit.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10772570582632336379">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer10202075655507541456">When I saw the names James M. McPherson and John Keegan, I thought this would be fun, but as I trudged through it, enjoying speculative paragraphs here and there, just to be mired in tangents about tactical and cultural what-ifs, I realized that what these guys are doing is describing fiction without actually telling a story.<br /><br />I guess this sort of thing is fun for a certain kind of mindset--the distant view of the chess board of history--but it became an exercise in frustration for me, who likes t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10202075655507541456">When I saw the names James M. McPherson and John Keegan, I thought this would be fun, but as I trudged through it, enjoying speculative paragraphs here and there, just to be mired in tangents about tactical and cultural what-ifs, I realized that what these guys are doing is describing fiction without actually telling a story.<br /><br />I guess this sort of thing is fun for a certain kind of mindset--the distant view of the chess board of history--but it became an exercise in frustration for me, who likes to try to get inside the heads of the history makers as much as I can. <br /><br /></span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer990635240">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17981328749119204670"><strong>What if...it were ALL written by top quality writers?</strong><br /><br />The premise of this book is explained by the title. The "What ifs...?" range in time from the failed Assyrian siege of Jerusalem in 701 B.C. to an extremely tense period in the Cold War in November of 1983. There are 40 different scenarios in all. For me, the most interesting were the scenarios concerning the Spanish conquest of the Aztecs and the American Revolution. Each of these had multiple "What ifs?" and I am now convinced that 2 of the</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17981328749119204670"><strong>What if...it were ALL written by top quality writers?</strong><br /><br />The premise of this book is explained by the title. The "What ifs...?" range in time from the failed Assyrian siege of Jerusalem in 701 B.C. to an extremely tense period in the Cold War in November of 1983. There are 40 different scenarios in all. For me, the most interesting were the scenarios concerning the Spanish conquest of the Aztecs and the American Revolution. Each of these had multiple "What ifs?" and I am now convinced that 2 of the luckiest men in all of history were Hernan Cortes and George Washington. They both could have failed in so many ways (13 are identified for Washington) and yet they persevered and had military success.<br /><br />An unintended "What if?" is the reader's inevitable comparison of these historian's writing styles. More than once I thought to myself, "What if this story was written by a better writer?" However, most were very good, especially the ones written by well-known historians Stephen Ambrose and James M. McPherson. <br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://dwdsreviews.blogspot.com/" target="_blank">http://dwdsreviews.blogspot.com/</a></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_148170003">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/148170003" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4970621-josh" class="left imgcol" title="Josh"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1297710392p2/4970621.jpg" alt="Josh" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/148170003?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 21, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4970621-josh" class="user" title="Josh">Josh</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer148170003">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13548999328001828912">Two scriptural passages come to mind while reading this collection of might-have-beens: First, from Alma, "by small and simple things are great things brought to pass" (Alma 36:6-7) and second, paraphrasing Nephi, the rise and fall of nations are in God's hands. (1 Ne. 17:37). In many of these essays, the fates of empires, both ancient and modern, turn on a lieutenant's reflexes or on the sudden vagaries of weather. The quality of writings varies among the selections, as might be expected in any</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13548999328001828912">Two scriptural passages come to mind while reading this collection of might-have-beens: First, from Alma, "by small and simple things are great things brought to pass" (Alma 36:6-7) and second, paraphrasing Nephi, the rise and fall of nations are in God's hands. (1 Ne. 17:37). In many of these essays, the fates of empires, both ancient and modern, turn on a lieutenant's reflexes or on the sudden vagaries of weather. The quality of writings varies among the selections, as might be expected in any compilation. The essayists also vary widely in the scope of their discussions, some getting caught up in details of a particular conflict, and others carrying the counter-factual through a series of suppositions to imagine how our world today might be different but for the crucial event. But interesting patterns emerge, including in the choice of historical moments--all seem to be pivotal in some way to the current rise and dominance of Western (and Anglo-American) civilization. Ultimately, playing "what if" becomes more than an amusing parlor game of speculation--you begin to apprehend the profound consequences of some very small things and may even catch a glimpse of a divine finger nudging history in a particular direction.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13548999328001828912">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_142658014">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/142658014" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4850367-sean" class="left imgcol" title="Sean"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1420135214p2/4850367.jpg" alt="Sean" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/142658014?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 26, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4850367-sean" class="user" title="Sean">Sean</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4850367-sean?shelf=non-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer142658014">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10731010910080623474">"What If? The World's foremost MILITARY Historians Imagine What Might Have Been" is a book with an interesting premise: to ask how certain historical events could have occurred differently. However, the book is wildly inconsistent, with some interesting, thought provoking articles outweighed by poorly conceived, poorly written, myopic ones.<br /><br />The book is a collection of articles edited by Robert Cowley, editor of "Military History Magazine" which explore various historical scenarios. The scenarios,</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10731010910080623474">"What If? The World's foremost MILITARY Historians Imagine What Might Have Been" is a book with an interesting premise: to ask how certain historical events could have occurred differently. However, the book is wildly inconsistent, with some interesting, thought provoking articles outweighed by poorly conceived, poorly written, myopic ones.<br /><br />The book is a collection of articles edited by Robert Cowley, editor of "Military History Magazine" which explore various historical scenarios. The scenarios, from the more generic (Robert E. Lee's Lost Orders, What if D-Day Failed, etc) to the more interesting (An Early Death for Alexander the Great, Success of the Spanish Armada) run over the course of five thousand years mostly encompassing events around Europe and the United States.<br /><br />A counterfactual is most interesting when it does two things: one, show how small things could have a large impact and two, what are the implications of these small change. Generally the articles address the first. However some have a tendency to get wrapped up in the minutia of the event themselves. At times the articles seem like history lessons in battle tactics with the "implications" tacked on cursorily at the end. Others seem to have written their articles in haste, like hastily put together response papers. A historian should not just have knowledge but be able to tell a story and a slight majority of the articles here are written by people who cannot construct a compelling narrative.<br /><br />The best articles are the ones that have a fine balance between history and speculation, create vivid scenarios and outcomes, and challenge some of our deepest held beliefs about history. My favorite in terms of premise was "The Repulse of the English Fireships: The Spanish Armada Triumphs" (Geoffrey Parker) because unlike most of the scenarios in the book, it speculates that just because the Spanish may have won does not mean that everything would change drastically from the current timeline. My other favorites included "Conquest Denied: The Premature Death of Alexander the Great" (Josiah Ober) for its vivid scenario/outcome and "If the Lost Order Hadn't Been Lost: Robert E. Lee Humbles the Union" (James M. McPherson), which is probably the best written in the bunch. The worst by far is "Our Midway Disaster: Japan Springs a Trap" (Theodore F. Cook, Jr.), an article which took considerable effort to get through before I just gave up. Another poor one is is "Ruler of the World: Napoleon's Missed Opportunities" (Alistair Horne) who, after going into many different scenarios neuters them by stating that the events probably wouldn't have changed given Napoleon's personality.<br /><br />The most important lesson to draw from this series of articles is that counterfactuals are interesting, they should not be written by just any historian. It takes ones of prodigious imagination and creativity to come up with interesting scenarios. In the end, this volume is hampered by its narrow focus on martial history and idle speculation. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10731010910080623474">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_107040034">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/107040034" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2009712-frank-sanello" class="left imgcol" title="Frank Sanello"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442449892p2/2009712.jpg" alt="Frank Sanello" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/107040034?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 14, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2009712-frank-sanello" class="user" title="Frank Sanello">Frank Sanello</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/434622.What_if_The_World_s_Foremost_Military_Historians_Imagine_What_Might_Have_Been" title="Paperback 9780641597596" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer107040034">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14568805543591530989">"What If?" belongs to a genre called alternate history in which the historian speculates how the past might have been altered if one or more events had or hadn't happened. One example: Britain would have been deprived of Winston Churchill's leadership which prevented the Nazi occupation of his country if the future prime minister had been killed instead of being injured when a taxi cab in New York in 1931 hit him as Churchill was crossing the street. <br /><br />This nonfiction history contains discrete ite</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14568805543591530989">"What If?" belongs to a genre called alternate history in which the historian speculates how the past might have been altered if one or more events had or hadn't happened. One example: Britain would have been deprived of Winston Churchill's leadership which prevented the Nazi occupation of his country if the future prime minister had been killed instead of being injured when a taxi cab in New York in 1931 hit him as Churchill was crossing the street. <br /><br />This nonfiction history contains discrete items (that's "discrete" as in self-contained or stand-alone, not "discreet" as in tactful or confidential. The two words may be the most incorrectly used adjectives in the English language). So, if one historical incident in the book doesn't interest you, you can still enjoy the book by reading other entries that do intrigue you.<br /><br />The book was recommended by a friend and fellow author after I told him I was writing an alternate history novel, "If Nazi Germany Had Won the War." I'm still working on the book, whose premise isn't as implausible as it sounds. <br /><br />In fact, as I wrote in a "Suite 101" magazine article<br /><br />(<a rel="nofollow" href="http://ww2history.suite101.com/article.cfm/if-hitler-had-won-world-war-ii--not-as-far-fetched-as-it-seems" target="_blank">http://ww2history.suite101.com/articl...</a>),<br /><br />if Hitler had made only three changes in his military strategy, a Nazi victory is not only plausible, but probable. <br /><br />(Teaser: Germany had developed a primitive atomic bomb and a jet-bomber capable of making the flight from Europe to the east coast of the U.S. without refueling in mid-flight. The technological innovations came to late to change the outcome of the war, but both the bomb and the bomber would have been available earlier if Hitler hadn't cut funding for research on the two projects. Hitler would have undoubtedly shared the technology with his ally, Japan. Germany would have dropped atomic bombs on America's east coast and Japan would have destroyed American cities on the west coast. The unthinkable effects of a nuclear holocaust would have prompted the U.S to surrender, just as our atom bombing of Hiroshima and Nagasaki finally persuaded the Japanese emperor to accept the Allies' demand for unconditional surrender.)<br /><br />Happy reading!<br /><br />Frank Sanello<br />FSanello@aol.com</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14568805543591530989">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1251311562">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1251311562" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/34214876-dom-watkins" class="left imgcol" title="Dom Watkins"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1408738641p2/34214876.jpg" alt="Dom Watkins" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1251311562?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 10, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/34214876-dom-watkins" class="user" title="Dom Watkins">Dom Watkins</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1251311562">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12694279280734453042">A fascinating series of essays positing alternative outcomes to some of histories most significant military events.<br /><br />Using counter-factual scenarios the writers envisage how, what may have appeared at the time, to be small scale occurrences are rendered significant if the outcome is altered.<br /><br />The book contains essays ranging from 701 BCE (The siege of Jerusalem by Sennacherib, King of Assyria) up to 1946 (Chiang Kai-shek halts the Nationalist assault on a Communist North China allowing them to re-gr</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12694279280734453042">A fascinating series of essays positing alternative outcomes to some of histories most significant military events.<br /><br />Using counter-factual scenarios the writers envisage how, what may have appeared at the time, to be small scale occurrences are rendered significant if the outcome is altered.<br /><br />The book contains essays ranging from 701 BCE (The siege of Jerusalem by Sennacherib, King of Assyria) up to 1946 (Chiang Kai-shek halts the Nationalist assault on a Communist North China allowing them to re-group and defeat the Nationalists 3 years later).<br /><br />Having just read Ian Morris' Why The West Rules For Now where Morris suggests that it is 'maps not chaps' and vast impersonal forces with patterns in migration, disease, famine, war and climate change that determine geopolitical events, this book instead, whilst covering these issues at times, focusses on how the smallest action or decision by an individual can have global influence.<br /><br />To pick an example: If Alexander the Great had not been saved by his bodyguard Cleitus at the age of 22 in the battle of Granicus as he made his first conquests of the Persian empire, then Hellenism would not have been exported from the Greek peninsula. Darius may not have then met a suitably organised fighting force to repel the Persian army and been able to sweep his forces through Europe combining East and West and changing the course of history. Had Alexander's General's descendants not been in a position to promote the language, literature, education and athletic tastes of the Greeks in the conquered lands then there may have been no Roman empire, no Jesus of Nazareth, no Judeo-Christian-Islamic religions. All because a bodyguard missed a thrust with his spear...<br /><br />There are some interesting ideas in the book and convincing cases made for how a single decision, action or weather condition can truly alter the course of history.<br /><br />Excellent read for anyone interested in Military History.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12694279280734453042">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1071644329">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1071644329" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/892400-bap" class="left imgcol" title="Bap"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" alt="Bap" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1071644329?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 04, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/892400-bap" class="user" title="Bap">Bap</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/892400-bap?shelf=military-history" class="actionLinkLite">military-history</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/892400-bap?shelf=history" class="actionLinkLite">history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1071644329">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14614807189594786320">This book is a compilation of essays from eminent historians like Seven Sears, john Keegan, David McCullough who imagine what might have been with a difference of the weather or one fact changing. The mongols had swept through Eastern Europe in the 13th century and were poised to sweep through Western Europe when word reached their armies that their ruler, the supreme Kahn had died which required the leaders of the invasion force to return to Mongolia to elect a new leader. The dark ages would h</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14614807189594786320">This book is a compilation of essays from eminent historians like Seven Sears, john Keegan, David McCullough who imagine what might have been with a difference of the weather or one fact changing. The mongols had swept through Eastern Europe in the 13th century and were poised to sweep through Western Europe when word reached their armies that their ruler, the supreme Kahn had died which required the leaders of the invasion force to return to Mongolia to elect a new leader. The dark ages would have been darker still if the mongols had conquered Europe, a fate averted by a single man's death.<br /><br />Or take The fate of Washington's army defeated in the Battle of Long Island and trapped with the British fleet seemingly in full control of the sea. A nor'easter however impedes the British from moving up the East River and when Washington slipped away over to Manhattan(another island where he should have been bagged) an evacuation aided by heavy fog which did not burn off till 11 am.<br /><br />Or the blunder of the Germans to weaken the thrust into France in 1914 in order to shift several divisions to the East for fear of the consequences of a Russian mobilization that was far quicker than anticipated. The Germans sorely needed those missing troops and might have knocked out France during the first six months of the war causing the Brits and Russia to sue for peace. That might have spared not only millions of deaths, averted the rise of Hitler 20 years later and saved Russia from a Bolshevik revolution. Yes Europe would have been dominated by Germany but isn't that what happened anyway?<br /><br />The book gets you thinking, "what if".</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14614807189594786320">...more</a>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1461592665">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15011774504700863516">Good, but not quite what I expected. I (wrongly) interpreted the title as license for various historians to indulge in alt-history fiction. I should not have been surprised to see historians doing what they do best, writing and interpreting history. The contributors to each section provide excellent sketches of the historical ecosphere around key turning points, musing only briefly over what might have occurred had things gone differently. Once these crucial events are identified, there is littl</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15011774504700863516">Good, but not quite what I expected. I (wrongly) interpreted the title as license for various historians to indulge in alt-history fiction. I should not have been surprised to see historians doing what they do best, writing and interpreting history. The contributors to each section provide excellent sketches of the historical ecosphere around key turning points, musing only briefly over what might have occurred had things gone differently. Once these crucial events are identified, there is little meat left on the bone for those that were waiting for fictional, alternate histories. It was a better read once I rid myself of such expectations. I found the section on the Revolutionary War (penned by David McCullough) the most engrossing. Alistair Horne's examination of Napoleon and his legacy's connections to the wars of the 20th Century was also excellent.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1121560959">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1121560959" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5890695-bill-glover" class="left imgcol" title="Bill Glover"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1394836648p2/5890695.jpg" alt="Bill Glover" /></a>

<span id="freeTextContainer3760466743268962947">Christ that took forever to read! I normally don't read military history because it is the study of complete diplomatic failure, and often doesn't concern itself with the social, economic, political pre-context that I find more interesting. It also flies a bit to close to the irresistible light bulb of human glory and valor.<br />I thought this one would be interesting because of it's seemingly anti-deterministic thesis, but it was kinda meh. Long ago I was discussing Marvel comic's What If title with</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3760466743268962947">Christ that took forever to read! I normally don't read military history because it is the study of complete diplomatic failure, and often doesn't concern itself with the social, economic, political pre-context that I find more interesting. It also flies a bit to close to the irresistible light bulb of human glory and valor.<br />I thought this one would be interesting because of it's seemingly anti-deterministic thesis, but it was kinda meh. Long ago I was discussing Marvel comic's What If title with someone who complained that they just kill off Spiderman in too many issues. In these essays there is plenty of 'if this, then no American hegemony' and a couple 'if this, than not Judeo-Christian religious hegemony'. Eh, so what? Every age has it's share of evangelizing mad men and the same percentage of people looking for something to believe in. How different would it be if the Brits didn't get thrown off of their colonies here when they did? We'd be just like Canada? Wow!<br />I guess I would have liked a bit more imagination, than 'we'd all be speaking German!!!!!'.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2560856355651666286">L’ucronia e’ esercizio da romanziere, non certo da storico. Eppure questo libro affida perlopiu’ proprio a degli storici il compito di creare storie alternative, con risultati estremamente disuguali. Non sorprendentemente, il capitolo piu’ convincente e’ quello sulle possibili alternative che Napoleone avrebbe potuto scegliere, trattato da un grande storico quale Alistair Horne; il piu’ deludente uno tra i pochi non affidati ad uno storico (Lewis Lapham), che osa affrontare Teutoburgo - battagli</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2560856355651666286">L’ucronia e’ esercizio da romanziere, non certo da storico. Eppure questo libro affida perlopiu’ proprio a degli storici il compito di creare storie alternative, con risultati estremamente disuguali. Non sorprendentemente, il capitolo piu’ convincente e’ quello sulle possibili alternative che Napoleone avrebbe potuto scegliere, trattato da un grande storico quale Alistair Horne; il piu’ deludente uno tra i pochi non affidati ad uno storico (Lewis Lapham), che osa affrontare Teutoburgo - battaglia sulla quale del resto si sono gia’ sprecati fiumi di inchiostro - senza peraltro un severo approfondimento del contesto post-augusteo (legga Heather, invece di fermarsi a Gibbon, che adoro ma che scriveva nel XVIII secolo). Altrettanto scontato l’errore capitale di tanti storici militari, che se nel contesto ucronico si attengono fin troppo scrupolosamente alla regola della riscrittura minima, dimenticano l’esistenza di fatti virtuali del secondo ordine, a causa dei quali lo schema “reale” spessissimo si impone comunque, al di la’ della riscrittura di un singolo evento.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1052470075">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1052470075" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/26632545-antoine-fleury-gobert" class="left imgcol" title="Antoine Fleury-gobert"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1387146956p2/26632545.jpg" alt="Antoine Fleury-gobert" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1052470075?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 12, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/26632545-antoine-fleury-gobert" class="user" title="Antoine Fleury-gobert">Antoine Fleury-gobert</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/568014.What_If_Military_Historians_Imagine_What_Might_Have_Been" title="Paperback 9780330487245" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/26632545-antoine-fleury-gobert?shelf=default" class="actionLinkLite">default</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1052470075">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10467057281207489719">This is not a fiction book. If you are looking for uchronia, this book is not for you. But if, on the contrary, you want a solid account of some of history weak points, or if you want to write an alternative or parallel history, this is a must-have.<br /><br />In this book, historians and war specialists try to highlight some of the weakest points in human (mostly western) history, trying to find where and when the current western European then American domination of the world would have been upturned. And</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10467057281207489719">This is not a fiction book. If you are looking for uchronia, this book is not for you. But if, on the contrary, you want a solid account of some of history weak points, or if you want to write an alternative or parallel history, this is a must-have.<br /><br />In this book, historians and war specialists try to highlight some of the weakest points in human (mostly western) history, trying to find where and when the current western European then American domination of the world would have been upturned. And decidedly, they are successful. One short example : while the first so-called <a rel="nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor">"European leader"</a> was at war with the papacy, the <a rel="nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mongol_Empire">greatest Empire known to man</a> was on the verge of conquering Europe. And it seems, according to this book, and very convincingly, that it could have succeeded, as such great was the difference in strategical thinking between Mongol and European generals. But History, as written by western scholars, mainly retains Frederick II, not Ogedaï.<br /><br />This book is well-written, not a complete boring scholarly account, but with references that one can check. Its title is a little misdirecting : it should be called "When are the 'What if?' of history", because no speculation is done on the consequences of the alternatives, except if theses 'what-ifs' changes the way a war was conducted. <br /><br />One must understand: this book is not about alternatives history, it is about possible outcomes of battles and wars. So, in my opinion, this book is a must-have if you want to understand the fragility of history. This book is a must-have if you want ideas on points of inflection in history (and write a parallel history). And is not a must-have if you are looking to fiction.<br /><br />PS: in my quality of Frenchman, I just have a little critic: the chapter on Napoleon Bonaparte is atrociously biased. Na!</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10467057281207489719">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6911841925412606678">I thought it was a solid book throughout. The best word to sum it up is fascinating. It is a collection of essay's by some historical authors from before BC to how China became a communist country. Some of the more important topics include if the Mongols were successful invading Europe there would be no Renaissance Era, the Greek cultural and contributions and Christianity would not exist. There were countless times when the US was never going to become independent during the Revelation. The onl</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6911841925412606678">I thought it was a solid book throughout. The best word to sum it up is fascinating. It is a collection of essay's by some historical authors from before BC to how China became a communist country. Some of the more important topics include if the Mongols were successful invading Europe there would be no Renaissance Era, the Greek cultural and contributions and Christianity would not exist. There were countless times when the US was never going to become independent during the Revelation. The only chance them for to win was because of George Washington. If the US did not enter WWI and have the Allies win then there probably wouldn’t be a French nation and at the same time Hitler would have never raised to power. If Japan did not surrender to the US, then Japan would probably be a communist country with the Soviets taking occupation there after WWII. <br /><br />This is a great read and if anyone likes history or ever wondered how things would have turned out differently, this is a book for you.<br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2807134145584979886">Un saggio molto carino e ben fatto che forse non riscuoterà il riscontro dei più che vedono la storia come una cosa noiosa, ma a me è sempre piaciuta e ho trovato questo libro davvero interessante e lo propongo volentieri a tutti per farli avvicinare a questa branchia del sapere che come disse qualcuno è bene ricordarlo ogni tanto "chi non conosce il proprio passato, non ha un presente".   <span style="display:none" id="freeText2807134145584979886">Un saggio molto carino e ben fatto che forse non riscuoterà il riscontro dei più che vedono la storia come una cosa noiosa, ma a me è sempre piaciuta e ho trovato questo libro davvero interessante e lo propongo volentieri a tutti per farli avvicinare a questa branchia del sapere che come disse qualcuno è bene ricordarlo ogni tanto "chi non conosce il proprio passato, non ha un presente". Mi riccollego anche ai fumetti americani dove il "What If..." è fonte di innumerevoli storie nell'universo Marvel. L'idea anche di ipotezzire come certi eventi avrebbero potuto cambiare radicalmente il nostro mondo è geniale. La cosa che fa più riflettere in questo libro è come il "caso" ha sempre giocato un ruolo fondamentale nella storia dell'umanità. Stephen King nella sua saga della "Torre Nera" (di cui presto pubblicherò una recensione visto che sono arrivato alla lettura del settimo e ultimo libro), fa ampio uso di ciò, definendo "Ka" il destino e come tutto ruota intorno ad esso. Curioso e bello.<br /></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_339379894">

  <a href="/user/show/5803604-wise-owl" class="left imgcol" title="Wise_owl"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1310840321p2/5803604.jpg" alt="Wise_owl" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/339379894?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 17, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5803604-wise-owl" class="user" title="Wise_owl">Wise_owl</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer339379894">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7857830290906645791">I read this book back in university and than it left a larger impression on me than it did this time around. I suspect this is largely the difference in reading a book as a undergraduate and after years of historical education and developing ones own historical perspective.<br /><br />Regardless, this book is about one of my favourite things; Uchronia. Taking history and examining the 'What ifs' of History. In this case mostly military History. What if Hitler had Invaded Great Britain. What if the Lost Orde</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7857830290906645791">I read this book back in university and than it left a larger impression on me than it did this time around. I suspect this is largely the difference in reading a book as a undergraduate and after years of historical education and developing ones own historical perspective.<br /><br />Regardless, this book is about one of my favourite things; Uchronia. Taking history and examining the 'What ifs' of History. In this case mostly military History. What if Hitler had Invaded Great Britain. What if the Lost Order had not been Lost. What if Britain had stayed out of WWI. Stretching from the Ancient period right up to the Cold War, this book has dozens of scenario's, some typical(the every popular suppositions on WWII and the civil War) and some less so(Would Europe be better off if Britain had stayed out of the Great War? Did Stalin do the West a favour by not seriously pursuing the idea of a unified Neutral Germany?)<br /><br />I would say the book seems over-focused on Military history. I think it misses good chances to discuss the social-political circumstances that would surround particular events and circumstances, though given that the book sets out to cover military history, this is perhaps a bit of personal bias. I would have liked to see a few more alt histories that aren't based around Western History, or 'Western Impact' models(What if Cortez had died in South America and the Aztecs had Triumphed for exmaple).<br /><br />Over-all the book still remains interesting, and I wasn't sorry I reread it.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7857830290906645791">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2556367198681193573">'Marvellously entertaining as well as thought-provoking - the finest intellectual parlour-game around.' - Noel Malcolm, "Sunday Telegraph". "More What If?", the sequel to the acclaimed "What If?" examines history's most fascinating what-might have-beens. More of the world's leading historians, including Geoffrey Parker, Theodore K Rabb, Cecilia Holland and Caleb Carr postulate on what might so easily have been. Concentrating on the crucial and the seemingly insignificant, "What If? 2" is an ente</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2556367198681193573">'Marvellously entertaining as well as thought-provoking - the finest intellectual parlour-game around.' - Noel Malcolm, "Sunday Telegraph". "More What If?", the sequel to the acclaimed "What If?" examines history's most fascinating what-might have-beens. More of the world's leading historians, including Geoffrey Parker, Theodore K Rabb, Cecilia Holland and Caleb Carr postulate on what might so easily have been. Concentrating on the crucial and the seemingly insignificant, "What If? 2" is an entertaining and brilliantly provocative look at the way our world could easily have been. What if William hadn't conquered? What if the enigma code remained uncracked? And would this even matter if Lord Halifax had become Prime Minister rather than Churchill? This selection of alternative history is both provocative and stimulating and gives us a valuable insight into the way things could so easily have been. Praise for the "What If?" series include: "Anyone interested in...History in general will find it fascinating to read" - "Spectator". "Pure, almost illicit pleasure" - Andrew Roberts, "Sunday Telegraph". <span id="freeTextContainer6812117707650707648">I read this a few years ago and it was amazing. I would read it again if I ever came across it and that is saying a lot from me because I never read anything more than once (except for LOTR but that doesn't count). This is a series of essays written by world class historians where the goal is for them to make the smallest change in history possible and explore the huge impacts that the change would have. We're talking coin toss type changes that could have had catestrophic consequences to billio</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6812117707650707648">I read this a few years ago and it was amazing. I would read it again if I ever came across it and that is saying a lot from me because I never read anything more than once (except for LOTR but that doesn't count). This is a series of essays written by world class historians where the goal is for them to make the smallest change in history possible and explore the huge impacts that the change would have. We're talking coin toss type changes that could have had catestrophic consequences to billions of people.<br /><br />Considering the exceptionally high infant mortality rate around the time and place Jesus of Nazareth was born, what would the world be like today if Jesus had died as an infant?<br /><br />What if the wind was blowing the opposite direction on D-Day?<br /><br />What if Alexander the Great had died at 18 years of age, in a battle that in real life he came unbelieveably close to dying in?<br /><br />What if Alexander the Great had lived to the ripe old age of 75, had heirs and produced a stable empire that would last well beyond his death?<br /><br />After reading this book you'll really wonder at the impact a single human being can have on everything.</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer679969776">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer821787833056512657">I enjoyed this book greatly. In some parts the book was a recap of history I had already read, with a very nice emphasis on pivotal points. In other parts it was an introduction to key historical events that I was not as familiar with. Some of the what-if scenarios were hard to handle in a kind of emotional way. As a modern American it is literally painful to imagine suffering damaging losses at the hands of the Germans or Japanese in WWII, or losing to the South in the Civil War, or even worse,</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText821787833056512657">I enjoyed this book greatly. In some parts the book was a recap of history I had already read, with a very nice emphasis on pivotal points. In other parts it was an introduction to key historical events that I was not as familiar with. Some of the what-if scenarios were hard to handle in a kind of emotional way. As a modern American it is literally painful to imagine suffering damaging losses at the hands of the Germans or Japanese in WWII, or losing to the South in the Civil War, or even worse, being subjugated by England in the Revolutionary War. <br /><br />The contra-factual (or alternate history) scenarios are painful in a different way. Many involve a correction to a mistake or a pattern of behaviors that results in a more favorable outcome for the factual losers. The postulates then proceed along lines of 'rational' behavior on a go-forward basis resulting in some sort of alternative utopian outcome. I suspect that real contra-factual historical progressions would be just as sloppy as the pre-event and pos-event factual world. Unfortunately, to create those scenarios requires such chains of assumptions that they become alternate-reality fictions rather than postulated contra-factual progressions. <br /><br />But, I believe that the challenges of my personal pain in some of the scenarios, and the impossibility of building realistically chaotic forward-looking scenarios, are endemic to this sort of exercise. All in all, I really enjoyed the book, and would give it a 4.5 if fractional grades were allowed. I can't give it a 5 though, but I recommend it highly to anyone who enjoys history. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="821787833056512657">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_112319860">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/112319860" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1037969-jen" class="left imgcol" title="Jen"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1219802524p2/1037969.jpg" alt="Jen" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/112319860?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 10, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1037969-jen" class="user" title="Jen">Jen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer112319860">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16647028140034893137">I really like the concept of this book -- if an event in the past had happened differently, how would that change history? For example, what would have happened if the D-Day invasion had failed? However, I found these essays, written by prominent historians, to be very uneven. I felt that the authors needed a bit more direction. Here would be my rules, were I to edit a second series (the examples I give are not from the book):<br /><br />1. You must provide enough background knowledge so that a reader unfam</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16647028140034893137">I really like the concept of this book -- if an event in the past had happened differently, how would that change history? For example, what would have happened if the D-Day invasion had failed? However, I found these essays, written by prominent historians, to be very uneven. I felt that the authors needed a bit more direction. Here would be my rules, were I to edit a second series (the examples I give are not from the book):<br /><br />1. You must provide enough background knowledge so that a reader unfamiliar with that era of history understands the context of the event you chose.<br />2. But the whole essay shouldn't be background, with only a few paragraphs at the end describing the counterfactual world that results.<br />3. Once your counterfactual event has occurred, you must explain the motivation for the subsequent chain of events. For example, you can't just say, "D-Day fails and then Hitler invades England and then the United States ends WWII by calling a truce and is the only nation left free from Hitler's domination." You have to explain WHY Hitler would succeed in the invasion and WHY the United States would give up, etc. <br />4. It would also be interesting if you explored multiple strands of possiblities. Like if D-Day failed, the Allies might try again and succeed or they might try again and fail again, or they might declare a truce, etc.<br />5. But you can't take your counterfactual scenario too far, like, "If D-Dad failed, we'd all be speaking German." Really? No one else would ever stand up to Hitler? There would never be revolt from within or anything, no matter how much of his plan he succeeded with?<br /><br />So, cool concept, uneven execution.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16647028140034893137">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <a href="/user/show/11220897-paul" class="user" title="Paul">Paul</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/568014.What_If_Military_Historians_Imagine_What_Might_Have_Been" title="Paperback 9780330487245" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/11220897-paul?shelf=history" class="actionLinkLite">history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer752625775">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer358597269679844470">An interesting proposition at first, even for someone like me who usually has a strong aversion to "military history", looking at how the world may have turned out if a battle or war had taken a different turn. It is somewhat let down by the very mixed quality of each contributor's efforts.<br /><br />Some of the essays are fascinating and riveting, though rather a lot of them barely even bother to embrace the counterfactual aspect and just regurgitate what <em>did</em> happen rather than what <em>might have</em> happened, k</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText358597269679844470">An interesting proposition at first, even for someone like me who usually has a strong aversion to "military history", looking at how the world may have turned out if a battle or war had taken a different turn. It is somewhat let down by the very mixed quality of each contributor's efforts.<br /><br />Some of the essays are fascinating and riveting, though rather a lot of them barely even bother to embrace the counterfactual aspect and just regurgitate what <em>did</em> happen rather than what <em>might have</em> happened, kind of defeating the whole point of the book.<br /><br />Another thing that lets it down is the fact that it determines to view history from a very USA-centred, 'beware the beast from the east' standpoint, almost in a "hark at just how technologically retarded we would have been had the Ottomans/Persians/ any other non-white empire defeated those glorious Europeans" way.<br />This is on top of a very heavy preoccupation with events of the American war of independence and civil war - together contributing at least half a dozen long chapters - which I personally have little or no interest in.<br /><br />An interesting idea and an okay read but I wouldn't advise anyone to go out looking for this or to hunt it down.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="358597269679844470">...more</a>

          </span>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer18396051">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7340779511563976753">The premise of this book sparked my interest; the primary thing that kept me from enjoying it more is my own lack of knowledge. I never studied ancient history, so the scenarios based on those battles were difficult for me to fully appreciate. I'm fairly familiar with more modern history, but those scenarios were often too detailed for my taste. Spending 30 pages discussing every aspect of the revolutionary war or civil war or whatever war that could have gone differently quickly lost its spice.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7340779511563976753">The premise of this book sparked my interest; the primary thing that kept me from enjoying it more is my own lack of knowledge. I never studied ancient history, so the scenarios based on those battles were difficult for me to fully appreciate. I'm fairly familiar with more modern history, but those scenarios were often too detailed for my taste. Spending 30 pages discussing every aspect of the revolutionary war or civil war or whatever war that could have gone differently quickly lost its spice. I was more interested in the sociopolitical ramifications of those changes. In that sense the ancient history chapters were more my speed. I've never had much of an interest in war and fighting and tactics. I guess I should have known better than to pick up a book about those things. In my defense I can only say that I thought it would be different. My other thing primary complaint is that Crowley and many of the contributors to this book, while they may be scholars of military history, don't have a degree to lend credence to their claims of knowledge. That fact makes me innately suspicious of the whole thing.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7340779511563976753">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/8663458?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 23, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/171450-clinton" class="user" title="Clinton">Clinton</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>
